## Chapter 14: GIS Basics:




### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 14 of "A Comprehensive Guide to Geographic Information Systems". In this chapter, we will delve into the advanced techniques of GIS, building upon the fundamental concepts covered in the previous chapters. 

Geographic Information Systems (GIS) have become an indispensable tool in various fields, including urban planning, environmental management, and disaster response. The ability to analyze and visualize spatial data has proven to be a powerful tool in decision-making processes. However, as the complexity of the data and the tasks at hand increase, so does the need for advanced GIS techniques.

In this chapter, we will explore these advanced techniques, providing a comprehensive understanding of how they can be applied in real-world scenarios. We will cover a range of topics, from advanced data analysis methods to sophisticated visualization techniques. Each topic will be explained in detail, with examples and illustrations to aid in understanding.

Whether you are a seasoned GIS professional or a newcomer to the field, this chapter will provide you with the knowledge and skills you need to tackle more complex GIS tasks. So, let's embark on this journey of exploring advanced GIS techniques together.




### Section: 14.1 Spatial Interpolation:

Spatial interpolation is a fundamental technique in Geographic Information Systems (GIS) that allows us to estimate the value of a variable at unsampled locations based on known samples. This is particularly useful when dealing with spatial data that is not evenly distributed or when we need to fill in gaps in the data.

#### 14.1a Understanding Spatial Interpolation

Spatial interpolation is a process that involves estimating the value of a variable at unsampled locations based on known samples. This is achieved by interpolating the values between the known samples. The interpolated values are estimates and are not exact values. However, they provide a reasonable approximation of the true values.

There are several methods of spatial interpolation, each with its own advantages and disadvantages. Some of the most commonly used methods include:

- **Nearest Neighbor Interpolation**: This method assigns the value of the nearest known sample to the unsampled location. This method is simple and fast, but it can lead to large errors if the unsampled location is far from the nearest known sample.

- **Linear Interpolation**: This method assigns the value of the known sample that is closest to the unsampled location. The value at the unsampled location is then calculated as a linear combination of the values at the known samples. This method is more accurate than nearest neighbor interpolation, but it can still lead to large errors if the unsampled location is far from the known samples.

- **Inverse Distance Weighted Interpolation**: This method assigns the value of the known sample that is closest to the unsampled location, but the value at the unsampled location is calculated as a weighted average of the values at the known samples. The weights are determined by the inverse of the distance between the unsampled location and the known samples. This method is more accurate than linear interpolation, but it can be computationally intensive.

- **Kriging**: This method is a form of weighted least squares regression that assigns the value of the known sample that is closest to the unsampled location, but the value at the unsampled location is calculated as a weighted average of the values at the known samples. The weights are determined by a variogram, which is a measure of the spatial dependence between the known samples. This method is more accurate than inverse distance weighted interpolation, but it can be computationally intensive and requires a good understanding of the underlying data.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into each of these methods, discussing their principles, advantages, and disadvantages. We will also provide examples of how these methods can be applied in real-world scenarios.

#### 14.1b Types of Spatial Interpolation

As we have seen in the previous section, there are several types of spatial interpolation methods. Each of these methods has its own strengths and weaknesses, and the choice of method depends on the specific requirements of the task at hand. In this section, we will delve deeper into the different types of spatial interpolation methods and discuss their properties.

- **Nearest Neighbor Interpolation**: This method is simple and fast, but it can lead to large errors if the unsampled location is far from the nearest known sample. The nearest neighbor interpolation method assigns the value of the nearest known sample to the unsampled location. This method is particularly useful when the data is sparse and the errors introduced by this method are acceptable.

- **Linear Interpolation**: This method is more accurate than nearest neighbor interpolation, but it can still lead to large errors if the unsampled location is far from the known samples. Linear interpolation assigns the value of the known sample that is closest to the unsampled location. The value at the unsampled location is then calculated as a linear combination of the values at the known samples. This method is particularly useful when the data is sparse and the errors introduced by this method are acceptable.

- **Inverse Distance Weighted Interpolation**: This method is more accurate than linear interpolation, but it can be computationally intensive. Inverse distance weighted interpolation assigns the value of the known sample that is closest to the unsampled location, but the value at the unsampled location is calculated as a weighted average of the values at the known samples. The weights are determined by the inverse of the distance between the unsampled location and the known samples. This method is particularly useful when the data is dense and the errors introduced by this method are acceptable.

- **Kriging**: This method is more accurate than inverse distance weighted interpolation, but it can be computationally intensive and requires a good understanding of the underlying data. Kriging is a form of weighted least squares regression that assigns the value of the known sample that is closest to the unsampled location, but the value at the unsampled location is calculated as a weighted average of the values at the known samples. The weights are determined by a variogram, which is a measure of the spatial dependence between the known samples. This method is particularly useful when the data is dense and the errors introduced by this method are unacceptable.

In the next section, we will discuss how to choose the appropriate spatial interpolation method for a given task.

#### 14.1c Applications of Spatial Interpolation

Spatial interpolation is a powerful tool in Geographic Information Systems (GIS) that has a wide range of applications. In this section, we will explore some of the key applications of spatial interpolation.

- **Geological Mapping**: Spatial interpolation is used in geological mapping to estimate the distribution of geological properties across an area. For example, the interpolation of rock type or mineral content can help geologists understand the subsurface structure of an area. This can be particularly useful in mineral exploration, where the distribution of valuable minerals can be estimated from a limited number of samples.

- **Environmental Modeling**: Spatial interpolation is used in environmental modeling to estimate the distribution of environmental variables across an area. For example, the interpolation of temperature, precipitation, or soil properties can help environmental scientists understand the environmental conditions across a region. This can be particularly useful in climate modeling, where the distribution of climate variables can be estimated from a limited number of observations.

- **Remote Sensing**: Spatial interpolation is used in remote sensing to estimate the distribution of surface properties across an area from satellite imagery. For example, the interpolation of land cover type or vegetation density can help remote sensing analysts understand the surface properties across a region. This can be particularly useful in land change detection, where the distribution of land cover types can be estimated from a limited number of satellite images.

- **Urban Planning**: Spatial interpolation is used in urban planning to estimate the distribution of urban properties across an area. For example, the interpolation of population density or housing price can help urban planners understand the urban properties across a region. This can be particularly useful in urban development, where the distribution of urban properties can be estimated from a limited number of observations.

In each of these applications, the choice of spatial interpolation method depends on the specific requirements of the task at hand. For example, nearest neighbor interpolation might be suitable for sparse data in geological mapping, but it might not be suitable for dense data in urban planning. Therefore, it is important to understand the strengths and weaknesses of each spatial interpolation method to choose the most appropriate method for a given task.




#### 14.1b Performing Spatial Interpolation

Performing spatial interpolation involves several steps. These steps are generally applicable to all methods of spatial interpolation, but the specifics may vary depending on the method being used. The following are the general steps involved in performing spatial interpolation:

1. **Data Preparation**: The first step in performing spatial interpolation is to prepare the data. This involves loading the data into the GIS software and ensuring that it is in a format that can be used for interpolation. The data should include both the known samples and the unsampled locations where the values need to be estimated.

2. **Choosing an Interpolation Method**: The next step is to choose an interpolation method. As mentioned earlier, there are several methods available, each with its own advantages and disadvantages. The choice of method depends on the specific requirements of the project and the nature of the data.

3. **Applying the Interpolation Method**: Once an interpolation method has been chosen, it can be applied to the data. This involves calculating the interpolated values at the unsampled locations. The specific steps involved may vary depending on the method being used.

4. **Validating the Results**: After the interpolation has been performed, it is important to validate the results. This involves comparing the interpolated values with the known values to ensure that the interpolation has been performed accurately. If there are significant discrepancies, the interpolation may need to be refined or a different method may need to be chosen.

5. **Saving the Results**: The final step is to save the results. This involves storing the interpolated values in a format that can be easily accessed and used in the future. This may involve exporting the data to a file or storing it in a database.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into each of these steps, providing detailed instructions and examples for each. We will also discuss the advantages and disadvantages of each interpolation method in more detail.

#### 14.1c Applications of Spatial Interpolation

Spatial interpolation has a wide range of applications in Geographic Information Systems (GIS). It is used in various fields such as environmental science, urban planning, and public health. In this section, we will discuss some of the key applications of spatial interpolation.

1. **Environmental Science**: Spatial interpolation is extensively used in environmental science. It is used to estimate the distribution of environmental variables such as temperature, precipitation, and soil properties. For example, in climate modeling, spatial interpolation is used to estimate the temperature at unsampled locations based on known temperature values at sample locations. This allows for a more detailed and accurate representation of the climate.

2. **Urban Planning**: In urban planning, spatial interpolation is used to estimate the distribution of various urban variables such as population density, land use, and traffic flow. This information is crucial for planning and decision-making processes. For instance, spatial interpolation can be used to estimate the population density in an urban area based on known population values at sample locations.

3. **Public Health**: Spatial interpolation is also used in public health. It is used to estimate the distribution of health variables such as disease prevalence, air quality, and noise levels. This information is vital for understanding and addressing health issues in a given area. For example, spatial interpolation can be used to estimate the air quality at unsampled locations based on known air quality values at sample locations.

4. **Remote Sensing**: In remote sensing, spatial interpolation is used to estimate the values of various remote sensing variables such as land surface temperature, vegetation cover, and sea surface temperature. This allows for a more detailed and accurate interpretation of remote sensing data.

5. **Geology**: In geology, spatial interpolation is used to estimate the distribution of various geological variables such as rock type, mineral content, and seismic activity. This information is crucial for understanding and exploring geological resources.

In conclusion, spatial interpolation is a powerful tool in GIS with a wide range of applications. It allows for the estimation of values at unsampled locations based on known values at sample locations. This is particularly useful when dealing with spatial data that is not evenly distributed or when we need to fill in gaps in the data.




#### 14.1c Spatial Interpolation in ArcGIS

ArcGIS is a powerful GIS software that provides a wide range of tools for spatial analysis and interpolation. In this section, we will focus on how to perform spatial interpolation in ArcGIS.

##### 14.1c.1 Data Preparation

The first step in performing spatial interpolation in ArcGIS is to prepare the data. This involves loading the data into the software and ensuring that it is in a format that can be used for interpolation. The data should include both the known samples and the unsampled locations where the values need to be estimated. 

In ArcGIS, data can be loaded into the software in various ways. For instance, data can be imported from a file, a database, or a web service. The data can be in various formats, including shapefiles, geodatabases, and CAD files.

##### 14.1c.2 Choosing an Interpolation Method

Once the data is loaded into ArcGIS, the next step is to choose an interpolation method. ArcGIS provides several interpolation methods, including Inverse Distance Weighted (IDW), Kriging, and Spline. Each of these methods has its own advantages and disadvantages, and the choice of method depends on the specific requirements of the project and the nature of the data.

##### 14.1c.3 Applying the Interpolation Method

After choosing an interpolation method, it can be applied to the data in ArcGIS. This involves calculating the interpolated values at the unsampled locations. The specific steps involved may vary depending on the method being used, but generally involve setting up the interpolation parameters, running the interpolation, and reviewing the results.

##### 14.1c.4 Validating the Results

After the interpolation has been performed, it is important to validate the results. This involves comparing the interpolated values with the known values to ensure that the interpolation has been performed accurately. If there are significant discrepancies, the interpolation may need to be refined or a different method may need to be chosen.

##### 14.1c.5 Saving the Results

The final step is to save the results. This involves exporting the interpolated data from ArcGIS in a format that can be easily accessed and used in the future. The data can be saved as a shapefile, a geodatabase, or a CAD file, depending on the format that is most convenient for the project.

In conclusion, spatial interpolation in ArcGIS involves several steps, including data preparation, choosing an interpolation method, applying the method, validating the results, and saving the results. Each of these steps is crucial for performing accurate and reliable spatial interpolation.




#### 14.2a Introduction to Remote Sensing

Remote sensing is a powerful technique used in geographic information systems (GIS) to collect data from a distance. It involves the use of sensors to collect data about the Earth's surface, atmosphere, and other physical phenomena without making physical contact with the object of interest. This technique is particularly useful in GIS as it allows for the collection of data over large areas and at regular intervals, providing a comprehensive and up-to-date view of the Earth's surface.

#### 14.2a.1 Remote Sensing Sensors

Remote sensing sensors are devices that collect data about the Earth's surface from a distance. These sensors can be mounted on satellites, aircraft, or even unmanned aerial vehicles (UAVs). The data collected by these sensors can be used to create maps, monitor changes over time, and identify patterns and trends.

#### 14.2a.2 Types of Remote Sensing Data

Remote sensing data can be broadly categorized into two types: imagery and non-imager. Imagery data includes satellite images and aerial photographs, which provide visual information about the Earth's surface. Non-imager data, on the other hand, includes data on the Earth's atmosphere, topography, and other physical phenomena, which can be collected using various types of sensors.

#### 14.2a.3 Applications of Remote Sensing in GIS

Remote sensing has a wide range of applications in GIS. It is used in land use and land cover change analysis, disaster management, resource planning, and environmental monitoring, among other areas. For instance, remote sensing can be used to monitor deforestation, detect urban sprawl, and track changes in sea ice cover.

#### 14.2a.4 Challenges and Limitations of Remote Sensing

Despite its many advantages, remote sensing also has its limitations and challenges. For instance, the accuracy of remote sensing data depends on various factors, including the type of sensor used, the resolution of the data, and the conditions during data collection. Furthermore, remote sensing data can be expensive to collect and process, and it may not always be available for certain areas of the world.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the principles and techniques of remote sensing, and explore how it can be used in conjunction with other GIS techniques to solve complex spatial problems.

#### 14.2b Remote Sensing Techniques

Remote sensing techniques are the methods used to collect data about the Earth's surface from a distance. These techniques can be broadly categorized into two types: active and passive.

##### 14.2b.1 Active Remote Sensing Techniques

Active remote sensing techniques involve the use of sensors that emit energy towards the Earth's surface and measure the energy that is reflected or backscattered. This category includes techniques such as radar and LiDAR.

###### Radar

Radar (Radio Detection and Ranging) is a form of active remote sensing that uses radio waves to detect and track objects. In the context of GIS, radar can be used to collect data about the Earth's surface, including topography, vegetation, and other surface features. The radar signal is emitted towards the Earth's surface and the reflected signal is measured. The time delay and frequency shift of the reflected signal can be used to determine the distance and velocity of the surface features.

###### LiDAR

LiDAR (Light Detection and Ranging) is another form of active remote sensing that uses light in the form of a pulsed laser to measure variable distances to the Earth. LiDAR can be used to collect high-resolution 3D data about the Earth's surface, including topography, vegetation, and buildings. The laser pulse is emitted towards the Earth's surface and the reflected pulse is measured. The time delay of the reflected pulse can be used to determine the distance to the surface features.

##### 14.2b.2 Passive Remote Sensing Techniques

Passive remote sensing techniques involve the use of sensors that detect energy that is naturally emitted or reflected from the Earth's surface. This category includes techniques such as infrared and visible light imaging.

###### Infrared Imaging

Infrared imaging is a form of passive remote sensing that detects infrared radiation emitted from the Earth's surface. This radiation can be used to identify and classify surface features, including vegetation, water, and urban areas. Infrared imaging can be performed using various types of sensors, including mid-infrared instruments and hyperspectral imagers.

###### Visible Light Imaging

Visible light imaging is another form of passive remote sensing that detects visible light reflected from the Earth's surface. This light can be used to identify and classify surface features, including vegetation, water, and urban areas. Visible light imaging can be performed using various types of sensors, including cameras and imaging spectrometers.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the applications of these remote sensing techniques in GIS.

#### 14.2c Applications of Remote Sensing

Remote sensing techniques have a wide range of applications in geographic information systems (GIS). These applications span across various fields, including environmental monitoring, disaster management, urban planning, and resource management.

##### Environmental Monitoring

Remote sensing plays a crucial role in environmental monitoring. It provides a cost-effective and efficient way to collect data about the Earth's surface, including vegetation, land use, and topography. For instance, satellite imagery can be used to monitor changes in vegetation cover over time, which can be indicative of deforestation, urbanization, or other environmental changes. Similarly, radar and LiDAR can be used to collect high-resolution 3D data about the Earth's surface, which can be used to study topography and other surface features.

##### Disaster Management

Remote sensing is also used in disaster management. It can provide timely and accurate information about the affected areas, which can be crucial for rescue and relief operations. For instance, satellite imagery can be used to identify flood-prone areas, monitor flood extent, and assess damage after a flood event. Similarly, radar and LiDAR can be used to collect data about the affected areas, which can be used to plan and implement disaster management strategies.

##### Urban Planning

In the context of urban planning, remote sensing can be used to collect data about the urban environment, including building density, land use, and transportation networks. This data can be used to plan and manage urban growth, identify areas for development, and assess the impact of urbanization on the environment. For instance, satellite imagery can be used to identify urban sprawl, monitor changes in building density, and assess the impact of urbanization on vegetation cover.

##### Resource Management

Remote sensing is also used in resource management. It can provide data about the Earth's resources, including mineral deposits, water bodies, and vegetation cover. This data can be used to plan and manage resource extraction, assess the impact of resource extraction on the environment, and identify areas for conservation. For instance, satellite imagery can be used to identify areas with high vegetation cover, which can be indicative of areas with high biodiversity. Similarly, radar and LiDAR can be used to collect data about the Earth's surface, which can be used to identify areas with mineral deposits.

In conclusion, remote sensing is a powerful tool in GIS, with a wide range of applications. Its ability to collect data from a distance makes it a cost-effective and efficient method for data collection. However, it is important to note that remote sensing data should be used in conjunction with other data sources to ensure accuracy and reliability.




#### 14.2b Remote Sensing Techniques

Remote sensing techniques are the methods used to collect and analyze remote sensing data. These techniques can be broadly categorized into two types: active and passive.

#### 14.2b.1 Active Remote Sensing Techniques

Active remote sensing techniques involve the use of sensors that emit energy towards the Earth's surface and then measure the energy that is reflected or backscattered. This technique is particularly useful for measuring the topography of the Earth's surface, as the reflected or backscattered energy can provide information about the surface's elevation. One example of an active remote sensing technique is radar, which emits radio waves towards the Earth's surface and measures the time it takes for the waves to bounce back, providing information about the surface's elevation and roughness.

#### 14.2b.2 Passive Remote Sensing Techniques

Passive remote sensing techniques involve the use of sensors that detect energy that is naturally emitted or reflected by the Earth's surface or atmosphere. This technique is particularly useful for measuring the temperature of the Earth's surface or atmosphere, as different wavelengths of energy are absorbed or reflected depending on the temperature. One example of a passive remote sensing technique is infrared spectroscopy, which measures the amount of infrared energy that is reflected or emitted by the Earth's surface or atmosphere, providing information about the temperature.

#### 14.2b.3 Hyperspectral Imager for the Coastal Ocean (HICO)

The Hyperspectral Imager for the Coastal Ocean (HICO) is a passive remote sensing instrument that collects data in 128 spectral bands from approximately 353 nm to 1080 nm wavelengths at 5.7 nm spectral resolution. This instrument is particularly useful for studying the coastal ocean, as it provides high-resolution data about the water's color, which can be used to estimate the concentration of phytoplankton, dissolved organic matter, and other properties.

#### 14.2b.4 Mid-Infrared Instrument (MIRI)

The Mid-Infrared Instrument (MIRI) is a passive remote sensing instrument that collects data in 10 filters from approximately 5.6 μm to 28 μm wavelengths. This instrument is particularly useful for studying the Earth's atmosphere, as it can detect the presence of water vapor, carbon dioxide, and other gases that affect the Earth's climate.

#### 14.2b.5 Advanced Very-High-Resolution Radiometer (AVHRR)

The Advanced Very-High-Resolution Radiometer (AVHRR) is a passive remote sensing instrument that collects data in five visible and near-infrared bands and 11 shortwave infrared bands. This instrument is particularly useful for studying the Earth's surface, as it can detect changes in vegetation, cloud cover, and other properties.

#### 14.2b.6 Visible Infrared Imaging Radiometer Suite (VIIRS)

The Visible Infrared Imaging Radiometer Suite (VIIRS) is a passive remote sensing instrument that collects data in 22 visible and infrared bands. This instrument is a follow-on to the Moderate Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer (MODIS), and it is particularly useful for studying the Earth's surface and atmosphere, as it provides high-resolution data about the Earth's properties.

#### 14.2b.7 Sentinel-2

Sentinel-2 is a satellite imaging system that collects data in 13 spectral bands from approximately 443 nm to 1080 nm wavelengths at 120 m spatial resolution. This system is particularly useful for studying the Earth's surface, as it can detect changes in vegetation, cloud cover, and other properties.

#### 14.2b.8 Surveying

Surveying is a technique used to collect data about the Earth's surface. This technique has been used for centuries, and it involves the use of instruments such as theodolites, total stations, and Real-Time Kinematic (RTK) Global Navigation Satellite Systems (GNSS) to measure the position and elevation of points on the Earth's surface.

#### 14.2b.9 21st Century Surveying

In the 21st century, surveying techniques have been improved and modernized. The use of remote sensing and satellite imagery has become more commonplace, allowing for more efficient and accurate data collection. Prominent new technologies include three-dimensional (3D) scanning and the use of lidar for topographical surveys. UAV technology along with photogrammetric image processing is also appearing, providing even more advanced methods for data collection.
